8.1.1 Fundamental Harmonic States and Their Angles

The quantization of charge and the nature of particles are a direct consequence

of the discrete, harmonic nature of the vector field. The harmonic states are

redefined as single numbers where the integer part represents the stable "space"

component and the decimal part represents the associated "time" component.

This is functionally the same as a ‘\[space, time\]‘ pair and explains how much

time is associated with the space.

• The Electron (Negative Charge): The electron exists as a stable,

resonant structure at the 1.75 harmonic state. This represents one unit of

space associated with 0.75 units of time.

• The Positron (Positive Charge): The positron, as the electron’s antiparticle,

exists at the 1.25 harmonic state, representing one unit of space

associated with 0.25 units of time.

• The Annihilation State (Unstable): When an electron and positron

annihilate, their components combine to form a momentary, unstable state

of 2.0. This represents two units of space associated with one full unit of

unstable time (‘1.75 + 1.25 = \[1, 0.75\] + \[1, 0.25\] = \[2, 1.0\]‘). A massive

particle structure cannot exist stably at this level; it is the domain of pure,

propagating force.

• Symmetric Decay into Two Photons: Since the unstable 2.0 state

cannot hold, it must decay. The most symmetric and stable way for it to

release its energy is to split perfectly in half. It decays into two components,

each with an energy corresponding to the 1.5 harmonic.

• Conclusion: The theory predicts that when an electron (1.75) and a

positron (1.25) annihilate, their combined energy forms a temporary unstable

state (2.0) which immediately decays into two photons. These photons

are not arbitrary; they are manifestations of the 1.5 harmonic, the

most stable, neutral energy state (one unit of space associated with 0.5

units of neutral, propagating time). This provides a deep, mechanical

reason for the production of two 0.511 MeV photons in electron-positron

annihilation—it is the system resolving into two perfectly balanced 1.5

components.

8.2 The Condition for Stability and Decay

The stability of a particle is not absolute but depends on the structure of its

harmonic state and its total mass-energy. This explains why some configurations

are fleeting while others are effectively eternal.

57

• The Duality of the 2.0 Harmonic: The harmonic state of 2.0 (representing

two units of space and one full unit of time) is fundamentally

unstable for low-mass systems, as seen in electron-positron annihilation

where it immediately decays. However, this same state becomes the condition

for stability if the mass of the system is sufficiently large.

• Stability through Fractal Depth: For a large-scale composite particle

(like a heavy nucleus), the stable state is not a simple integer. It becomes a

configuration of the form 2.000...1..., where there is an effectively infinite

number of decimal places (‘0‘s) representing a vast buffer of opposing

vectors before reaching the final, infinitesimal "Cost of Being" (‘CoB‘)

unit.

• The Mechanism of Decay: This vast "time value" (the ‘.000...1...‘ part)

is what makes the particle stable. However, it is used for the ‘CoB‘ at every

Planck time tick. The particle remains stable as long as this buffer exists.

When the ‘CoB‘ has finally eroded the entire buffer, the particle becomes

completely unstable and disperses.

• The Stability of Fractional Harmonics: This explains why particles

based on fractional harmonics like 1/3 and 2/3 (quarks), or repeating

decimals like 0.555..., are fundamentally stable. Because their decimal

representation is infinite, they possess an infinite time buffer to be used

for the ‘CoB‘. Their half-life is therefore effectively infinite, making them

stable constituents of matter.

8.3 The Law of Half and Quantization

The existence of these discrete harmonic states is a direct consequence of the

Law of Half.

• The Law of Half: Due to the Law of Fractals, everything must fit within

itself or be divisible by half. Those entities or interactions that are unable

to divide by half effectively will emit that remainder. The harmonic bands

(0.25, 0.5, 0.75, 1.0) are the halves and relative equal divisions of 1, with

each further division becoming more unstable.

• Quantization through Fractal Division: This implies that the very

act of quantization—of the contiguous vector field resolving into discrete,

stable entities—occurs through a process of fractal division, specifically

by halving. The stable harmonic states are the points where this recursive

halving process finds stable equilibrium.

• Harmonics as Perfect Opposition: A harmonic state is not merely

a pattern; it is a configuration where the internal opposing vectors and

potentials within the field are in a state of perfect, balanced, and stable

tension. This "perfect opposition" allows for the formation of coherent,

self-sustaining structures.

58

9 Further Deductions: The Intrinsic Dynamics of

Reality

This section details advanced deductions within the Vector Field Theory, explaining

the intricate relationships between fundamental properties, the nature

of particles, and the mechanics of interaction, building upon the foundational

principles previously established.

9.1 The Nature ofWaves: Wavelength, Energy, and Flicker

The properties of waves (2D vector balls) are intrinsically linked to the dynamics

of their internal stress and energy.

• Wavelength and Internal Stress: The wavelength (λ) is the total

spatial extent of one complete cycle of the wave’s asymmetric strain, conceptualized

as the length of its "left" (l) and "right" (r) interaction, so

λ = l + r.

• Exponential Spatial Decay of Internal Stress: The stress within a

wave (or particle) follows an Exponential Spatial Decay profile. This

decay is caused by the force of each individual vector within the field.

The stress is highest at the core and diminishes exponentially outwards.

As a wave gets shorter/longer, its exponential spatial decay bands would

shrink/expand accordingly.

• Frequency as Decay Rate: Waves flicker because of the specific energy

value’s exponential spatial decay ratio. Relative to c, this exponential

spatial decay ratio effectively represents the wave’s frequency.

• Wave Flicker: The flickering observed in waves is the dynamic manifestation

of the wave’s internal energy distribution as it propagates, continuously

paying its "Cost of Being" (CoB) at each Planck-scale step.

9.2 Particle Composition and the Accumulation of Waves

Matter particles are composite structures formed from the accumulation of constituent

waves, with their properties arising from these compositions.

• Inverse Exponent Addition Rule: When a wave is caught in a particle

(vector ball), it increases the particle’s inverse exponent decay rate by 1.

This means the total decay rate of a composite particle is the sum of the

decay rates of its constituent waves.

9.3 The Origin of Temporal Shearing Force and Incompatibility

The Vector Field Hypothesis posits that the difficulty for waves of different

frequencies to combine is due to a "temporal shearing force" arising from their

geometric imbalance.

• The Geometric Analogy: Particles with charge (0.25 and 0.75 harmonics)

have an asymmetric, oval-like shape. A neutral particle (0.5 harmonic)

is a perfect sphere. The "radius" of a charged particle is therefore more

like an oval, reflecting its internal asymmetry.

• Temporal Shearing Force: When two waves of different frequencies

(different "time phases") attempt to interact, their mismatched internal

dynamics create a force that causes one to "slip" or "slide" past the other,

analogous to a physical shearing force. This "temporal shearing force" is

a direct consequence of the mismatch between their oscillation rates.

• Slipping of the Smaller Wave: The smaller wave (with its slower,

more diffuse internal dynamics) is more susceptible to this shearing force.

It finds it hard to "stick" to the larger, more energetic wave and is repelled.

This provides a direct, mechanical link between a particle’s geometric shape

(its "oval radius") and the temporal forces it experiences, explaining why interactions

and the formation of stable composites are dictated by a principle of

harmonic compatibility.

9.4 Compton Wavelength and Schwarzschild Radius: Refined

These two fundamental length scales are reinterpreted in the Vector Field Theory

to describe the particle’s internal wave-like composition and its external

gravitational effect, respectively.

• Schwarzschild Radius (rS): Describes the innermost, most compressed

compression and its effect as a particle in space. This is the

"accumulated high stress opposition area" where the vector field is maximally

compressed, and time is most concentrated.

• ComptonWavelength (λC): Describes the spatial extent or "reach"

of the particle’s internal composite waves and electromagnetic influence. It

represents the range of its average excess vectors in the magnetic ranges,

describing how many Planck lengths over c it influences. This is also

directly related to the rate at which the particle’s internal vector field

"flickers" or undergoes its fundamental "wobble" (Zitterbewegung). The

wavelength is the energy radiating out from the radius.

10 The Nature of Particles and the Origin of

Mathematics

10.1 The Physical Origin of Mathematics

This model proposes that mathematics is not an abstract tool used to describe

the universe but is an emergent property of the universe’s fundamental geometry.

62

To be able to describe itself, the universe first invented mathematics, driven by

the Law of Opposition, as a vector is opposed it creates the bounds (−∞ + 1)

and (+∞− 1) and all quanta in between.

• The Law of One: The absolute minimum cost for existence is (−∞+1).

• The Law of Half: Due to the Law of Fractals nature, everything must fit

within itself or be divisible by half, those that are unable to divide by half

effectively will emit that remainder. The harmonic bands are the halves

and relative equal divisions of 1, with each further division becoming more

unstable.

• The Law of Integers: A stable composite "vector ball" can only be

formed from a precise integer number of constituent waves. A configuration

with a fractional component (e.g., "half a wave") is geometrically

incomplete and cannot form a closed, stable shell. It would instantly disintegrate.

The very existence of stable matter is the physical proof that

nature "counts" in whole numbers.

• The Law of Fractions: The type of stable particle that can be formed

is determined by the fundamental harmonic of the waves being used. The

existence of distinct, stable particle families at specific energy ratios is the

physical origin of fundamental fractions.

• The Origin of Circles and Spheres: The geometric shape of a circle

is the natural, emergent result of a perfect harmonic. When constituent

waves interact with perfect temporal symmetry (same time frequency),

they create a structure where energy is distributed with perfect spatial

symmetry around a center. The equation (x − h)2 + (y − k)2 = r2 is a

direct description of this physical shape. The stability of this structure is

guaranteed when the geometry itself is quantized, which occurs when r2

resolves to an exact whole number. Particles are spherical because their

constituent waves are in perfect, quantized harmony. The model provides

a mechanical explanation for why stable particles are spherical, based on

the interaction of their constituent waves.

• The Relativity of Whole Numbers and Quantization: What constitutes

a "whole number" is not absolute, but is relative to the chosen

scale of observation and the fundamental units of resolution defined by

the vector field itself. The Planck time (tP ) and the fundamental volume

of observable space (c2) act as the primary "pixel size" or "frame" for our

observable reality. Quantities that appear as fractions at our macroscopic

scale could resolve into "whole" or complete units when viewed at a more

granular, sub-Planck scale (scaling inwards). This implies that the very

concept of integers and fractions emerges from the field’s intrinsic mechanism

of discretizing its continuous potential into defined entities at specific

resolution thresholds.

63

• The Origin of Arithmetic Operations:

– Addition and Subtraction (Normalization): These operations

arise from the fundamental tendency of vectors to "normalize themselves"

or seek equilibrium when combined. When two vectors interact,

their combined state resolves into a new, stable configuration.

This process of finding a resultant vector, whether by combining

(addition) or opposing (subtraction), is the physical basis for

these arithmetic operations. It represents the field’s intrinsic drive

towards balance and definition.

– Multiplication and Division (Dimensional Extension): These

operations are the "squared" or higher-dimensional extensions of addition

and subtraction. Just as a 2D plane emerges from 1D lines,

multiplication and division represent interactions that extend across

multiple dimensions (e.g., creating areas or volumes). For instance,

multiplying two lengths (L1 × L2) yields an area (L2), which in the

theory corresponds to a 2D planar effect. Extending this to 3D, multiplication

could represent the volumetric strain of the field. These

operations are therefore rooted in the field’s ability to form increasingly

complex, higher-dimensional configurations.

10.2 The Geometric Origin of Circular Structures and

Mass-Energy Duality

The model provides a mechanical explanation for why stable particles are

spherical, based on the interaction of their constituent waves. The geometric

shape of a circle is the natural, emergent result of a perfect harmonic.

When constituent waves interact with perfect temporal symmetry (same

time frequency), they create a structure where energy is distributed with

perfect spatial symmetry around a center. Particles are spherical because

their constituent waves are in perfect, quantized harmony.

10.2.1 The Mechanism of Sphericity

The spherical nature of stable particles arises from a process of symmetric

energy equalization between interacting constituent waves (2D vector

planes).

– The Interaction: When two constituent waves intersect, their internal

dynamics interact.

– The Mechanism of Spillover: As the internal ’x’ space values

of one wave fill with their time components, the energy must be

conserved. To maintain stability, this excess must "spill over" into

the perpendicular ’y’ space of the other wave.

64

– Symmetric Equalization: Since the ’y’ space wave is simultaneously

undergoing the same process and spilling into the ’x’ space,

the most stable and symmetric state of equilibrium they can achieve

is a circular or spherical standing wave. This provides the physical

"why" for the circle formula being a fundamental descriptor of stable

particle geometry.

10.2.2 The Circle Formula as a Stability Condition

The equation (x − h)2 + (y − k)2 = r2 is a direct description of this

physical shape. The stability of this structure is guaranteed when the

geometry itself is quantized, which occurs when r2 resolves to an exact

whole number.

– The Fractal Circle Formula and Interacting Vector Planes:

The circle formula describes two 2D vector planes interacting. In

this context, x represents the energy of one vector and y represents

the mass. The terms h and k represent the width (or inherent base

frequencies) of the perpendicular 2D vectors within each 2D vector

plane. Following this, due to the Law of Fractals and Opposition,

each individual plane is also comprised of an x and a h plane.

– Conceptual Proof: Harmonic vs. Non-Harmonic Interactions:

We can demonstrate how the circle formula distinguishes between

stable (harmonic) and unstable (non-harmonic) interactions.

∗ Harmonic (Stable) Interaction: An interaction leading to a

perfectly stable, unit-level particle structure, where r2 resolves

to a whole number (e.g., r2 = 1). For example, if we set the

interacting components such that (x −h) ≈ 0.707 and (y −k) ≈

0.707, then r2 = (0.707)2 +(0.707)2 ≈ 1. This indicates a stable

geometric configuration.

∗ Non-Harmonic (Unstable) Interaction: An interaction where

the components do not perfectly align, resulting in a fractional

r2 value (e.g., r2 = 1.5). For example, if (x − h) ≈ 0.866 and

(y − k) ≈ 0.866, then r2 = (0.866)2 + (0.866)2 ≈ 1.5. This

indicates an unstable configuration that cannot form a closed,

stable shell and would emit the "remainder" to achieve a stable,

whole-number state.

– The Circle Formula as Mass-Energy Duality: The equation

(x−h)2 +(y −k)2 = r2 is reinterpreted as a fundamental statement

about the duality of mass and energy, representing a "conservation

of state" for a stable particle.

∗ ‘x‘ as Energy: The ‘x‘ component represents the particle’s dynamic,

propagating, wave-like nature. It is the "time component"

of its existence.

65

∗ ‘y‘ as Mass: The ‘y‘ component represents the particle’s stable,

self-contained, particle-like nature. It is the "space component"

of its existence.

∗ Conservation of State: The equation as a whole signifies that

for any stable particle, there is a fundamental trade-off between

its "wave-ness" (‘x‘) and its "particle-ness" (‘y‘). The sum of

these two properties must always equal a constant, stable value

(r2), creating a state of perfect, circular equilibrium.

10.4 The Meaning of E=mc2 in the Vector Field Theory

Unlike the derivation above which calculates the energy of a 2D wave

(photon) from its geometric properties, E = mc2 in the Vector Field

Theory is primarily a statement of fundamental equivalence and

relationship between matter and spacetime, rather than a derivation in

the same step-by-step mathematical way.

In this model:

– E (Energy): Represents the intrinsic "vector power" or total dynamic

activity of a 3D matter particle’s (fermion’s) vector field.

This is the sum of its internal vector forces in all directions (x, -x, y,

-y, z, -z).

– m (Mass): Fundamentally is the physical compression/displacement

that a particle’s existence imposes on the spacetime field. This compression,

and thus the very definition and stability of m, is dependent

on and maintained by the "inwards pressure from outside

sources" – the collective gravitational influence of all other

matter in the universe. This also implies that the "no 0 energy"

principle (the field always having a value \> 0) is what allows for

mass.

– c (Local Speed of Light): This c in the equation represents the

size of observable 2D spacetime

Therefore, E = mc2 signifies that the energy (E) inherent in a 3D

matter particle’s dynamic vector field is equivalent to the spacetime

compression (m) it manifests as mass, where both that

mass’s stability and the local speed of light (c) are fundamentally

shaped and defined by the particle’s dynamic relationship

with the rest of the universe’s matter. It’s a profound statement

about the energetic cost and manifestation of existence within a relational,

self-defining universe, rooted in the constant activity of the vector field.

10.6 Geometric Angle (θ) for Photons of Different Energies

Let’s calculate the geometric angle (θ) for three different types of photons,

from low energy to high energy, based on their frequency.

The Core Principle & Formula: A wave’s frequency (f) is determined

by its fundamental geometric angle (θ) relative to our observable reality

(c-space). The formula derived for this relationship is:

f =

sin(θ)

tP

Where tP is the Planck Time, approximately 5.39 × 10−44 seconds. We

can rearrange this to solve for the angle θ for any given frequency:

sin(θ) = f · tP

Now, let’s apply this to our three examples.

10.6.1 Example 1: Radio Photon (Low Energy)

Let’s take an FM radio wave, a very low-energy photon.

– Frequency (fradio): A typical FM frequency is 100 MHz, which is

1 × 108 Hz.

Calculation:

sin(θradio) = (1 × 108 Hz) × (5.39 × 10−44 s)

sin(θradio) = 5.39 × 10−36

Resulting Angle: Since sin(θ) is extremely small, the angle θ (in radians)

is approximately the same value.

θradio ≈ 5.39 × 10−36 radians

70

This is an incredibly small, almost flat angle. According to this model,

this means a radio wave is a disturbance that is barely "tilted" into our

observable reality, spending the vast majority of its existence outside our

time phase.

10.6.2 Example 2: Visible Light Photon (Medium Energy)

Now let’s look at a photon of green light, which has much more energy.

– Frequency (fvisible): Approximately 5.6 × 1014 Hz.

Calculation:

sin(θvisible) = (5.6 × 1014 Hz) × (5.39 × 10−44 s)

sin(θvisible) ≈ 3.02 × 10−29

Resulting Angle:

θvisible ≈ 3.02 × 10−29 radians

While still incredibly small, this angle is over 10 million times larger than

the angle for the radio wave. This demonstrates a clear relationship: as

the particle’s energy and frequency increase, its geometric angle into our

reality also increases.

10.6.3 Example 3: Gamma Ray Photon (High Energy)

Finally, let’s take a very high-energy gamma ray.

– Frequency (fgamma): A high-energy cosmic gamma ray can have a

frequency of 1 × 1020 Hz or more.

Calculation:

sin(θgamma) = (1 × 1020 Hz) × (5.39 × 10−44 s)

sin(θgamma) = 5.39 × 10−24

Resulting Angle:

θgamma ≈ 5.39 × 10−24 radians

This angle is another 100,000 times larger than the angle for visible light.

71

10.6.4 Conclusion on Geometric Angle:

These calculations provide powerful support for this model. They show

that:

– The hierarchy of energy is a hierarchy of geometry. Higher energy

photons have a larger geometric angle (θ) into our observable space.

– Our reality is barely being "touched." The fact that even the most

energetic particles we’ve ever observed have an infinitesimally small

angle θ implies that the vast majority of the vector field’s potential

action is occurring outside our direct perception.

– The Planck Frequency is the limit. A hypothetical particle with θ =

90◦ would have sin(θ) = 1, giving it a frequency of 1/tP ≈ 1.85×1043

Hz. This is the Planck frequency, the absolute maximum frequency,

representing a vector oscillating entirely within our c-space. This

model naturally provides this ultimate upper limit.

10.8 The Quantization of Charge from First Principles

The quantization of electric charge is a direct consequence of the discrete,

harmonic nature of the vector field, which manifests in two distinct ways

depending on the nature of the interaction.

– The Planck Volume as the Arena: All fundamental particle

interactions and formations occur within the context of a Planck

volume.

– Harmonic Attractor States: The vector field within this volume

resists arbitrary deformation. When energy is applied, the field will

always seek equilibrium by "snapping" to a stable harmonic state.

– Two Modes of Quantization: The specific stable harmonics that

emerge depend on the type of interaction:

∗ Quarters (External Interaction): For interactions involving

a single particle and the external spacetime field (a process

of vector addition), the stable harmonics are based on quarters

(0.25, 0.5, 0.75), arising from the Law of Half. This governs the

charges of leptons.

73

∗ Thirds (Internal Interaction): For interactions within a composite

system (like quarks in a proton), the process is an equalization

between internal volumes, bounded by the 2c interaction

threshold. This different boundary condition forces a different

quantization, resulting in stable harmonics based on thirds

(1/3, 2/3).

– Charge as the Harmonic State: The observable charge of a particle

is a direct manifestation of which harmonic state its constituent

fields have resolved into:

∗ Charge 0 (Neutral): A field that resolves to the 0.5 harmonic

is in a state of perfect geometric balance. This results in a neutral

particle like the Higgs boson.

∗ Charge -1 (e.g., Electron): A field that is forced into the 2/3

harmonic creates a stable particle with an inherent geometric

"surplus." This asymmetry manifests as a fundamental unit of

negative charge, or −1/3 of required charge to equalize.

∗ Charge +1 (e.g., Positron): A field that resolves to the 1/3

harmonic creates a stable particle with an inherent geometric

"deficit." This asymmetry manifests as a fundamental unit of

positive charge, or +2/3 to equalize.

∗ Fractional Charges (Quarks): The charges of +2/3 and −1/3

for quarks are a direct result of their internal, relational interactions

resolving into the stable harmonic states based on thirds.

– Conclusion: Charge is not a continuous variable; it is quantized

because the underlying geometry of the vector field has different discrete,

stable harmonic solutions depending on the type of interaction.

Antimatter and the Mechanism of Annihilation

The Vector Field Theory provides a new, mechanical explanation for the

– Antimatter as "Negative Addition": Antimatter is not merely

a particle with an opposite charge. It is a vector configuration that is

fundamentally opposite in its temporal construction. While matter

is formed by a "0 + 1" positive vector addition, antimatter is a "0 −

1" negative vector addition, representing the opposite way to add

numbers.

– The Third Option (Universal Addition): When matter (0 + 1)

and antimatter (0−1) meet, their opposing signs cancel out resolving

into the universal addition Annihilation is a unique outcome, distinct

from the physical addition (+) or subtraction (−) of our reality. The

‘+‘ and ‘−‘ of matter and antimatter do not simply cancel out to

76

zero; their opposition instantly resolves into a state of equality,

directed process; it is a fundamental a-causal event that bypasses the

normal progression of time and instantly results in a state of perfect

cancellation at the ultimate boundary of our reality.

– Decay into Photons: The emitted photons (gamma rays) are the

energy released from both masses equalling a density 2c space, or

2c mass, however this perfect mass becomes instantly unstable as

it represents a particle’s space suddenly being added by the difference

between its mass and 2c mass, mimicking the initial state and

reinforcing the fractal genesis of the universe

This provides a direct, physical mechanism for annihilation, where the

act of opposition drives the vector field to its maximum observable limit,

transforming matter and antimatter into pure, propagating energy.

– Matter-Antimatter Asymmetry: The observed dominance of

matter in the universe is not a result of a broken law, but a consequence

of the initial, random stochastic nature of the vector field’s

quantization. The universe simply had a slight statistical preference

for ‘+‘ additions over ‘-‘ additions over the course of the moments

after genesis, the fact is neither are negative or positive but they

define each other, matter is formed from ‘0+1‘ because it is more numerous

relative to antimatter, leading to the surplus of matter that

constitutes the cosmos we see today.

10.11 Quarks, Gluons, and Composite Particles

– The Nature of Quarks: Quarks are the fundamental "electrical"

particles, representing "stable-unstable" flavors of the vector field.

Their defining characteristic is an inherent imbalance between their

internal "space" and "time" components.

– The Strong Force and Gluons: The force that binds quarks together

(the strong nuclear force) is a direct manifestation of their

Schwarzschild radius (the volumetric temporal mass). The regions

of interaction between these radii, which are mediated by the

quarks’ Compton Wavelengths (the planar temporal mass), are

what we perceive as gluons.

– Composite Particles as States of Matter: The combination of

quarks can result in different states of matter. Charged particles

(like protons) are composite systems where the quarks’ timespace

imbalances do not cancel out.

– The Neutron as a Phase Change: The neutron represents a

fundamentally different, neutral state of matter. It is formed when

constituent quarks undergo a phase change, creating a new "stablestable"

state where their individual time wells combine to form a

perfectly balanced, composite time well, thus neutralizing their electrical

nature.

78

– Proton Decay: A proton’s stability is not absolute. It is maintained

by the stabilizing "inwards pressure" from the rest of the universe,

delivered by a constant bombardment of gravity waves of all scales

(from observable down to 0.0...1ℵU). A proton could only decay if

it were traveling away from everything else at a relative speed of ‘c‘,

becoming causally disconnected from this stabilizing influence. In

this state, it becomes its own isolated frame of reference. While it

no longer feels the stabilizing external forces, it must still oppose

the "Cost of Being" (0.0...1ℵU). This unopposed internal tension

forces the proton’s composite time well to lose cohesion, decaying by

continuously shedding its energy as these unresolved units, which we

perceive as neutrinos. From the neutron’s perspective in this state,

if it could look "behind" itself, it would perceive the entire universe

from that single angle or face.

10.12 Neutrinos

Neutrinos are not fundamental particles in the same way as electrons.

They are the direct, observable manifestations of sub-electron scale oscillations

of time.

– Mechanism: The "neutrino" we detect is the tiny fraction of a stable,

sub-electron scale oscillation that exists within our specific time

phase. They are effectively sub-relativistic effects made manifest.

– Neutrino Flavors and Oscillation: The different "flavors" of neutrino

(electron, muon, tau) correspond to the different fundamental

harmonic energies of these sub-waves. "Neutrino oscillation" is the

macroscopic effect of the particle constantly flickering between these

three stable sub-electron harmonic states as it propagates.

10.13 Quantum Gravity as the Limit of Bulk Gradient

The Vector Field Theory resolves the conflict between General Relativity

(GR) and Quantum Mechanics (QM) by positing that what we perceive

as "gravity" is a statistical, macroscopic emergent property that naturally

devolves into discrete quantum interactions at the Planck scale.

10.13.1 Gravity as a Statistical Emergence

In VFT, the "force" of gravity is defined as the 0.5 Harmonic Planar

Strain. This is a diffuse, pervasive strain on the vector field caused by

the existence of mass (which is itself a concentration of time).

79

– Macroscopic Scale (General Relativity): At large scales (planets,

stars), this strain is experienced as a smooth, continuous curvature

of spacetime. The vast number of underlying vector interactions

averages out into a predictable "bulk gradient." This is why GR works

perfectly for large masses: it describes the average slope of the field.

– The Breakdown of the Gradient: As one zooms in to the scale

of individual particles and Planck volumes, the "smoothness" of this

gradient begins to break down. The statistical average loses its meaning

when there are too few vectors to average.

"Quantum Gravity" is not a separate force to be discovered, but simply

the transition from the statistical domain to the discrete domain.

10.14.1 The Mechanics of Spin and Emission

Within this model, the intrinsic property of spin is not a classical rotation

but is a direct measure of how "timelike" a particle is. It is the

quantification of its "stable instability."

Spin is directly proportional to the deconstructive interference within the

particle’s vector field. This internal tension is a measure of how much

the particle’s configuration deviates from a perfectly stable, purely spatial

state, and thus how "timelike" (dynamic and wave-like) it is. By the Law

of Fractals, the amount of deconstructive interference required to define

matter is half c. This explains why fundamental particles exhibit fractional

spin—it is a direct measure of this inherent, stable instability rather than

a classical rotation.

The geometric foundation for these phenomena lies in interactions described

by formulas like the circle formula. The equation (x − h)2 + (y −

k)2 = r2 directly describes two 2D vector planes interacting. The stability

of the resulting geometric configuration is guaranteed only when r2

resolves to an exact whole number.

– Redefining Harmonic States: The fundamental harmonic states

are redefined as single numbers where the integer part represents

the stable "space" component and the decimal part represents the

associated "time" component.

– Electron (Negative Charge): Is represented by the state 1.75,

signifying one unit of space associated with 0.75 units of time.

– Positron (Positive Charge): Is represented by the state 1.25,

signifying one unit of space associated with 0.25 units of time.

81

– The Unstable State: Annihilation creates a momentary, unstable

state of 1.100 (or simply 2.0), representing two units of space

associated with one full unit of unstable time.

– Photon (Neutral): The stable decay product is represented by the

state 1.5, signifying one unit of space associated with 0.5 units of

neutral, propagating time.

Furthermore, The equation (x − h)2 + (y − k)2 = r2 describes the rate of

time we experience consciously to define scale, (x − h)n + (y − k)n = rn

where 0 \< n \< 2 can be used for any sub-planck time scale

If, however, an interaction leads to an r2 value that contains a fractional

component (i.e., it is not an exact whole number), the system becomes

unstable and must emit energy or particles to achieve equilibrium. This

emission process is not fixed to a specific harmonic; rather, the emitted

remainder can be anywhere relative. For instance, if an interaction results

in an unstable configuration equivalent to r2 = 1.6, the fractional

remainder of 0.1 is effectively re-scaled to 0.100 and, per the Law of Fractals,

resolves itself into 0.05, representing the emission of a stable, deeply

quantized sub-harmonic energy unit. This occurs because the excess energy

now exists in the neighboring vector ball that seeks self-normalization

by resolving into 1.

Conclusion: The theory predicts that when an electron (1.75) and a

positron (1.25) annihilate, their combined energy forms a temporary unstable

state (2.0) which immediately decays into two photons. These photons

are not arbitrary; they are manifestations of the 1.5 harmonic, the

most stable, neutral energy state. This provides a deep, mechanical reason

for the production of two 0.511 MeV photons in electron-positron

annihilation—it is the system resolving into two perfectly balanced 1.5

components.

Opposition, and the result of energy not being able to be destroyed in

to the formation of chemical bonds and the phenomenon of motion, emerge

vector fields through the intersection of their phase times.

11.4 The Origin of Relativistic Mass

The model provides a direct, mechanical explanation for the phenomenon

of relativistic mass increase, rooting it in the concept of "volumetric temporal

mass."

– The Foundation: A particle’s observable mass is its "volumetric

temporal mass"—the amount of "time" (or dynamic field activity)

that is concentrated and trapped within its stable 3D volume.

– The Energy of Motion: When kinetic energy is added to an object

to put it in motion, that energy is not purely external. It must be

incorporated into the particle’s total field configuration. This added

energy is the "inertial energy to escape its own volumetric bounds

into other spaces."

– The Consequence: This added kinetic energy increases the total

activity within the particle’s field. In the model’s terms, it literally

adds more "time" inside the particle’s volume, increasing its temporal

density.

– The Result: Because the particle now contains a higher concentration

of "time," its volumetric temporal mass has increased. An

external observer measures this increase as a greater resistance to further

acceleration, which we define as an increase in its relativistic

mass.

11.5 The Chemical Bond Equation

The energy released or absorbed in a chemical reaction is a direct consequence

of the system of particles settling into a new, more stable harmonic

state. The conceptual equation for the energy of a bond (ΔEbond) is:

ΔEbond = (EA + EB) − (NAB · Ewave · h)

Where:

– ΔEbond is the Bond Energy: The total energy released (exothermic)

or absorbed (endothermic) when the bond forms, which is the value

measured in chemistry.

– (EA + EB) is the Initial Energy: The total energy of the separate,

un-bonded atoms or molecules before they interact.

– (NAB · Ewave · h) is the Final Energy: The energy of the new, stable

molecule. This is determined by:

∗ NAB: The new integer number of constituent waves that make

up the final, combined molecule.

∗ Ewave: The base energy of the constituent waves involved.

∗ h: The Fundamental Harmonic (0.25, 0.5, or 0.75) that the new,

combined system has "snapped" into to achieve stability.

This equation shows that the strength of a chemical bond is not an arbitrary

value, but is directly determined by which fundamental harmonic

the new, combined system settles into.

11.6 Case Study: Nuclear Fusion

The energy released in nuclear fusion is a direct confirmation of this principle.

We can analyze the fusion of two Deuterium atoms into one Helium-4

atom:

23.84 MeV = (4.0282 u × 931.494 MeV/u) − (4.0026 u × 931.494 MeV/u)

– Initial State: Two Deuterium nuclei have a combined mass of 4.0282

u\.

– Final State: They fuse into a single Helium-4 nucleus, a much more

stable geometric configuration. Its mass is 4.0026 u.

– Energy Matchup: The "missing" mass of 0.0256 u is converted

into 23.84 MeV of energy. This measured value is the direct energetic

consequence of the system snapping into a deeper, more stable

harmonic state.

87

We can also analyze the fusion of two Helium-4 nuclei to form one Beryllium-

8 nucleus:

– Initial State: Two Helium-4 nuclei (alpha particles) have a combined

mass of 8.005206 u. This represents the total initial energy

content of the system.

– Final State: They fuse into a single Beryllium-8 nucleus, an unstable

configuration, with a mass of 8.005305 u. This represents the

total final energy content of the new, unstable system.

The energy change in this process, ΔEbond, can be explicitly shown using

the energy equivalents of the masses, consistent with the chemical bond

equation:

−0.091 MeV = (8.005206 u×931.494 MeV/u)−(8.005305 u×931.494 MeV/u)

The "missing" mass in this case is actually a mass increase of 0.000099 u,

meaning the reaction absorbs approximately 0.091 MeV of energy. This endothermic

nature, requiring energy input rather than releasing it, explains

the Beryllium-8 nucleus’s inherent instability, as it fails to snap into a

lower-energy, higher-equilibrium harmonic state. The unstable Beryllium-

8 nucleus produced in the first step typically fuses almost immediately

with another Helium-4 nucleus to form a stable Carbon-12 nucleus. This

second step is highly exothermic, demonstrating the universe’s drive towards

lower-energy, more stable configurations.

– Initial State: One Beryllium-8 nucleus and one Helium-4 nucleus

have a combined mass of 8.005305 u + 4.002603 u = 12.007908 u.

This represents the total initial energy content of this second stage.

– Final State: They fuse into a single Carbon-12 nucleus (12C), a

highly stable geometric configuration, with a mass of 12.000000 u

(by definition of the atomic mass unit). This represents the total

final energy content of the new, stable system.

The energy released in this process, ΔEbond, can be explicitly shown using

the energy equivalents of the masses, consistent with the chemical bond

equation:

7.367 MeV = (12.007908 u×931.494 MeV/u)−(12.000000 u×931.494 MeV/u)

The "missing" mass of 0.007908 u is precisely converted into 7.367 MeV of

energy. This measured value confirms that the system snaps into a significantly

deeper, more stable harmonic state by forming Carbon-12, resolving

substantial tension in the vector field and releasing a large amount of

energy.

88

11.7 The Condition for Interaction: Intersecting Phase

Times

For any interaction to occur—be it a force being felt, a photon being absorbed,

or a chemical bond forming—a fundamental condition must be

met: the constituent waves of the interacting particles must have intersecting

phase times.

– Duality and Contiguous Force: A single vector cannot be affected

in isolation; this is a statement of action-reaction at the most

fundamental level. For a force to be transmitted from one particle

to another, the 2D force-carrying wave (boson) must act as a bridge,

its own phase time intersecting with the phase times of at least two

vectors in the target particle. This is why force is transmitted contiguously

through the vector field.

– The Fourier Transform as a Physical Probe: The Fourier

Transform is not merely a mathematical tool; it is a direct description

of this physical process. When a physicist performs a "spectrum

sweep" on a particle, they are sending in waves of varying frequencies.

According to the theory, they are actually sending in waves with varying

time phase angles. The particle only absorbs or resonates with a

wave when the incoming wave’s time phase angle perfectly matches

the time phase angle of its own constituent waves. For all other frequencies/

angles, the particle is "blind," and the Fourier Transform

correctly returns a value of zero.

– Frequency as a Real Number: Because a wave’s geometric angle

(θ) relative to our c-space can be any value in a continuous range,

its frequency (f = sin(θ)/TP ) is also a continuous variable. This is

the physical origin of real numbers in mathematics. The single, realnumber

value of a wave’s frequency is a direct "shadow" or "projection"

of its entire, more complex geometric state.

11.8 The Mechanics of Light: Reflection and Refraction

The principles of optics emerge directly from the conditions for interaction.

– Refraction as Field Resistance: When a wave (a photon) passes

through a medium, it is forced to interact with the vector fields of that

medium’s atoms. Each interaction has the fundamental (−∞ + 1)

energy cost. This cumulative "drag" or "resistance" from the field

slows the wave’s effective propagation, causing its path to bend.

– Reflection as Failed Interaction: If an incoming wave is too weak

or its time phase is incompatible, it fails to meet the condition of intersecting

with multiple internal vectors simultaneously. It cannot

89

transfer its energy forward into the new medium. Instead, it is absorbed

and subsequently re-emitted by one of the surface vectors on

the next Planck time tick randomly due to the (−∞ + 1) cost. This

re-emission is what we observe as reflection.

11.9 The Origin of Chemistry: Harmonics and Bond

Strength

All of chemistry is the macroscopic process of charged (asymmetric) vector

balls seeking more stable, lower-energy configurations by equalizing their

– Chemical Bonds as Harmonic Equalization: A chemical bond

is a shared geometric arrangement between two or more atoms that

allows their combined vector fields to settle into a new, more stable

harmonic state.

– Bond Strength as a Harmonic State: The strength of the bond

is determined by which fundamental harmonic the new, combined

system resolves to. The system will always seek the lowest, most

stable harmonic possible.

∗ A very strong bond (like a covalent bond) is one where the

combined fields achieve a state of ultimate stability. This occurs

when they resolve into the deeply stable relative 1 harmonic,

harmonics equaling 1, which represents the ultimate achievement

of absolute baseline equilibrium for the bonding system, leading

to maximum stability. Similarly, resolution to the balanced 1

harmonic or even 0 also signifies a state of perfect coherence,

equality and unity, thus forming deeply stable bonds. These

deeply stable bonds arise from the powerful resolution of tension

between charged harmonics (0.25 and 0.75), but perfect alignment

required for such resolution can be less likely to form.

∗ A weaker ionic bond might represent a system that resolves

to a specific stable configuration, but along an easier, more

probable path to a general equilibrium, rather than achieving

the absolute deepest energy well.

∗ A very weak Van der Waals force would be the result of a

temporary, unstable resonance at the 0.75 harmonic, representing

very minimal tension resolution.

∗ Furthermore, neutral particles which fundamentally exist at

the 0.5 harmonic baseline, generally do not bond easily, as

they lack the inherent charge imbalance or tension that drives

the formation of chemical bonds.

– Conclusion: The entire spectrum of chemical bond energies is a direct

reflection of the discrete, stable harmonic states available within

90

the vector field, influenced by the drive towards ultimate equilibrium

A particle’s mass is a measure of the total energy or "vector power" of

For the smallest stable subatomic particle, such as the electron, while

its internal structure occupies a specific sub-Planck volume, its sphere of

strains in the surrounding field—is hypothesized to sum to 2p (2 Planck

units) in a spherical configuration. This 2p interaction volume reflects

the particle’s fundamental connection to the 2c interaction threshold of

the constituent waves. For composite particles, the total energy across

the vector ball is determined by the average Planck energy (or average

density) of all the Planck volumes that constitute it.

Redefining the Planck Mass In this model, the Planck Mass is not

merely a constant derived from other constants. It has a direct, mechanical

meaning: The Planck Mass is the maximum possible energy/mass that can

be contained within a single, stable Planck-scale volume before its internal

dynamics reach the 2c threshold. It is the ultimate "compressed Planck

volume." Any further addition of energy to this single point would breach

the asymptotic limit of coherent existence, causing it to form a black hole.

It represents the absolute upper limit for a stable "knot" in the vector

field.

11.11 The Harmonic Origin of Gravity and Electromagnetism

The theory provides a unified mechanical origin for the forces of gravity

11.11.1 Gravity as the 0.5 Harmonic Planar Strain

Gravity is not merely a passive "bulk strain" caused by mass; it is the

direct, physical manifestation of the universe’s most fundamental, stable,

and neutral resonant state: the 0.5 harmonic. It is the pervasive, planar

strain on the universe caused by the existence of this 0.5 harmonic.

– The Universal Carrier Wave: The 0.5 harmonic acts as the "carrier

wave" or the baseline equilibrium state of the entire vector field.

– The Origin of Mass Interaction: A particle possesses mass because

its structure is a stable excitation that necessarily includes or

couples to this fundamental 0.5 harmonic (via the Higgs mechanism).

Therefore, all massive particles both generate and are affected by this

harmonic, which we perceive as the universal force of gravity.

11.11.2 Electromagnetism as Planar Strain from Harmonic Difference

The electric and magnetic fields are not separate forces, but are specific,

ordered patterns or "modulations" imposed upon the underlying 0.5 harmonic

gravitational field. Coulomb’s constant (k) is the planar strain

caused by the difference between the 0.25 and 0.75 harmonics existing.

This signifies the precise measure of the tension or disequilibrium

created by the simultaneous presence of positive (0.25) and negative (0.75)

charged harmonics.

– The Electric Field (Static Polarization): A stationary charged

particle (e.g., an electron with a 0.75 harmonic component) creates

a static, radial polarization in the surrounding 0.5 harmonic field.

Its inherent geometric asymmetry imposes a permanent "push" or

"pull" on the neutral background. This sustained, polarized strain is

what we measure as an electric field.

– The Magnetic Field (Dynamic Rotation): Magnetism is the

dynamic effect created when a charged particle moves. A moving

charge "drags" and "twists" the background 0.5 harmonic gravitational

field, inducing a rotational or vortical strain. This circulating

strain pattern is what we perceive as a magnetic field.

11.11.3 Conclusion: A Unified Electromagnetic Field and the

Hierarchy Problem

This model perfectly explains why electricity and magnetism are two sides

of the same coin. They are simply the static (polarized) and dynamic (rotational)

strains imposed by an asymmetric particle on the single, underlying

reason for why only moving charges create magnetic fields and why an

electron ’hole’ or imbalance is required for electrical flow.

The vast difference in strength between gravity and electromagnetism (the

Hierarchy Problem) is explained by the nature of their planar strain.

Both Gravity and Electromagnetism are planar effects within the vector

field, and we can treat them as having the same dimensionless dimensions.

The hierarchy is fundamentally rooted in what each constant measures:

– Gravity (G) measures the pervasive planar strain of the 0.5 harmonic’s

existence, a subtle baseline equilibrium.

– Electromagnetism (k) measures the much stronger, localized planar

strain arising from the active difference between the 0.5 harmonic

This inherent tension and quantifiable "difference" is precisely what drives

the enormous disparity. The specific mechanism for k’s strength lies in

93

the Planck distance difference in these harmonic distortions (quantified as

11.12 The Four Fundamental Forces

The four forces of nature are not separate entities, but different modes of

– Electromagnetism (Polarized Strain): An electromagnetic field

It is a more focused, 2D planar effect outside of the 0.5 harmonic.

– Strong Nuclear Force (Interwoven Geometry): The Strong

– Weak Nuclear Force (Geometric Deficiency): The Weak Nuclear

Force is an emergent effect of the inherent, probabilistic flaws

in a composite particle’s geometry. It is the statistical likelihood

that the internal tumbling of a particle’s vectors will align in a selfdestructive

configuration, causing decay.

11.14 The Mechanism of Electrical Conduction and

Failure

The model provides a mechanical explanation for electrical current and

why materials like wires fail under high voltage.

– Electric Current as a Compression Wave: Electrical current is

not the simple movement of electrons. It is a propagating compression

wave that moves through the "sea" of compatible electron vector

fields within a conductive material.

– Voltage as Wave Intensity: Voltage is the measure of the intensity

of this compression wave. Adding voltage is equivalent to adding

more energy and mass, but specifically into the electromagnetic angular

configurations of the material’s vector fields.

– Instability and Failure: A material can only contain a finite amount

of this specific angular energy. When too much voltage is applied,

the compression wave becomes so intense that the "EM angles" of

the atoms in the wire are stressed beyond their point of stability.

– Vaporization as Catastrophic Radiation: The entire wire, now

in a state of maximum instability, must shed this excess energy to

obey the laws of conservation. It does so in a single, catastrophic

event: it releases exactly the amount of energy it was holding just

before failure by violently radiating its constituent particles away.

This is what we observe as the wire vaporizing.

11.15 The Origin of Material and Atomic Properties

– The Chemical BondWell: The stable, low-energy state that holds

multiple molecules together in a liquid or solid. It is a shared, stable

harmonic configuration between the molecules.

95

– The Molecule’s Own Vector Stability: The stability of a single,

– Boiling/Melting Points: These properties are a direct consequence

"bond well") and the stability of an individual molecule’s vector field.

The boiling point is the temperature at which the internal chaotic

energy of the molecules overcomes the energy of the bond well.

– Electrical Conduction and Failure: Electrical current is a propagating

compression wave through compatible electron fields. Voltage

is the intensity of this wave. A wire vaporizes when the voltage adds

too much angular energy, causing the entire system to become unstable

and catastrophically radiate the excess energy it was holding.

11.16 Motion, Instability, and Decay: A Unified Perspective

The model provides a unified mechanical explanation for how a particle

handles energy, where decay, emission, and fission are different perspectives

on the same fundamental process of resolving instability.

– The Two Options: When a particle’s internal vector field is put

under stress by an influx of energy, it has two options to resolve this

instability. Decay is understood as motion pushed to the point of

fundamental instability for that configuration.

– Option 1: Translation. The particle can translate its volumetric

compression of time through space. This is simple motion, where the

particle maintains its structural integrity.

– Option 2: Fission/Decay. If the energy influx is too great for the

particle to handle through simple translation, it is forced to decay.

It resolves its instability by fissioning into smaller, more stable compressions

of time, ejecting even smaller compressions of time into the

surrounding space. The velocity of these ejected particles is equal

to the excess time compression that was used to bring the parent

particle to its point of instability.

– Example - Laser Cooling: This is a perfect example of Option

1\. Photons are absorbed from one direction, imparting momentum.

The atom then re-emits a photon in a direction determined by the

relation between its spin and its mass, the direction of the plane

while its spinning and the time it takes for the photon to be absorbed

and emitted being its mass. Even though the energy absorbed and

emitted is the same, the directed absorption and emission result in

a net change in the particle’s translational motion. The atom is

"cooled" by essentially thrusting in all directions.

96

– Example - Light Emission: An excited atom is a system pushed

towards Option 2. The absorbed energy creates an internal instability

too great for simple translation. The atom is forced to decay

via planar fission, ejecting the excess energy as a planar packet of

compressed time (a photon) to return to a stable state.

– Example - Nuclear Fission: This is a large-scale version of Option

2\. A large nucleus like Uranium is pushed to its point of maximum

instability. It cannot resolve this through translation and is forced

to decay by fissioning into smaller, more stable compressions of time

(Krypton, Barium) and ejecting other smaller compressions of time

(neutrons, photons).

– The Mechanism of Emission and Randomness: The emission

of a particle (e.g., a photon) is not truly random. Its direction is

determined by the orientation of the parent particle at the moment

of emission. Because a matter particle’s spin is an inherent, chaotic

"wobble," its orientation is constantly and unpredictably changing.

The time it takes for an excited state to decay and emit a particle is

determined by the combination of its planar instability (the intensity

of its wobble/spin) and the number of Planck spaces it occupies (its

volumetric mass).

11.17 The Mechanism of Vector Interaction

When two or more vector fields interact, the force between them is not

a direct negation of their internal properties. Instead, it is an emergent

effect arising from their shared relationship with the universal boundary.

1\. Universal Opposition: Each interacting particle’s internal time

component is in a constant state of opposition against the universal

boundary of 2c time. This creates a fundamental tension for each

particle.

2\. The Remainder: When the two particles interact, the force between

them is the remainder or disequilibrium that results from their

combined opposition to the 2c boundary.

3\. The Resultant Force: This remainder then acts as the force that

opposes the two interacting volumes, creating the push or pull we

observe. The force is a consequence of their joint relationship with

the universe as a whole, not a simple transaction between the two

particles.

12 Deriving the CKM and PMNS Matrices

from Geometric Principles

The Standard Model of particle physics uses two "mixing matrices," the

CKM and PMNS matrices, to describe the probabilities of particles transforming

from one type to another via the weak force. These matrices

contain experimentally measured values that are not derived from first

principles. The Vector Field Theory provides a direct, mechanical origin

for these values, positing that they are not abstract probabilities, but

are a quantitative measure of the "geometric compatibility" and "phase

overlap" between the interacting particles.

12.1 The CKM Matrix (Quarks)

The CKM matrix describes the mixing of the six quarks. The weak force

allows up-type quarks (Up, Charm, Top) to transform into down-type

quarks (Down, Strange, Bottom), but not always within the same generation.

The CKM matrix quantifies the probability of these transformations.

12.1.1 The Standard Formulation

The matrix is written as:

Vud Vus Vub

Vcd Vcs Vcb

Vtd Vts Vtb

Each element, \|Vij \|2, represents the probability of a quark of type i transforming

into a quark of type j.

12.1.2 Interpretation within the Vector Field Theory

In this theory, the CKM matrix elements are a direct measure of the geometric

compatibility between the "vector balls" of the respective quarks.

– High Probability (\|Vud\| ≈ 0.974): The geometries and internal

harmonics of the Up quark and Down quark vector balls are very

similar. Their shapes are highly compatible, like a key that fits a

lock almost perfectly. Therefore, the probability of the weak force

successfully reconfiguring one into the other is very high.

– Low Probability (\|Vub\| ≈ 0.004): The geometries of the Up quark

and the Bottom quark are very dissimilar. Their internal harmonic

structures do not overlap well. The weak force interaction is geometrically

suppressed, making the transformation extremely rare.

98

The CKM matrix is therefore the universe’s "engineering specification"

for how well the different quark geometries can be added, subtracted, or

reconfigured.

12.2 The PMNS Matrix (Leptons)

The PMNS matrix describes the phenomenon of neutrino oscillation. A

neutrino is created with a specific "flavor" (electron, muon, or tau), but

it travels as a mixture of three different "mass states" (ν1, ν2, ν3). The

PMNS matrix quantifies this mixture.

12.2.1 The Standard Formulation

The matrix relates the flavor states to the mass states:

Ue1 Ue2 Ue3

Uμ1 Uμ2 Uμ3

Uτ1 Uτ2 Uτ3

Each element, \|Uαi\|2, represents the probability that a neutrino of flavor

α is measured to have the mass of state i.

12.2.2 Interpretation within the Vector Field Theory

This matrix provides a perfect framework for the "Sub-Electron Particle

Zoo" predicted by the theory.

– Mass States as Harmonics: The three mass states (ν1, ν2, ν3) are

the three stable, neutral particles predicted by the theory at the subelectron

energy scale (e.g., the ‘ 2.088 eV‘, ‘ 1.392 eV‘, and ‘ 0.696

eV‘ particles).

– Flavor States as a Mixture: The "flavor" of a neutrino is not a

fundamental state, but a superposition of the three underlying mass

harmonics. An "electron neutrino" is a specific mixture of the three

stable harmonic states, a "muon neutrino" is a different mixture, and

so on.

– Oscillation as Phase Interference: Neutrino oscillation is the

macroscopic effect of the constituent waves of these three mass harmonics

going in and out of phase with each other as the neutrino

propagates. The PMNS matrix elements are a measure of the geometric

overlap between the flavor states and the underlying mass

harmonics, dictating the frequency and amplitude of this oscillation.

99

12.3 Conclusion

The CKM and PMNS matrices are not abstract mathematical constructs.

They are direct, physical descriptions of the geometric compatibility and

harmonic overlap between the "vector balls" that constitute matter and

the probability of emission or absorption. In effect, they are the calculators

for the universe, computing the various charge layers and density

for particles. As evident by the matricies themselves, they describe the

inverse harmonic, shape and charge for two intersecting waves, the Up row

being the perpendicular of the Left column

13 Derivation of Fundamental Constants and

Force Dynamics

13.1 The Observable Drag Constant and the Speed of

Light

The theory posits that the measured speed of light (c) is not an arbitrary

number, but a direct consequence of a fundamental "drag" or "resistance"

from the vector field itself. The speed of light we observe is the result of

an ideal propagation speed being constantly reduced by the (−∞ + 1)

opposition from 2c space, the ideal propagation speed exceeding 2 seconds

per second, resolving into the ideal speed of 3 or (∞).

13.1.1 Calculation of the Drag Constant

We can calculate this "Universal Drag Constant" by finding the difference

between a theoretical, ideal speed of light and the measured value.

– Ideal Speed (cideal): The theoretical, unhindered speed of propagation

in the vector field. We define this as exactly 3 × 108 m/s.

– Measured Speed (cmeasured): The actual speed of light in a vacuum,

which is defined as 299, 792, 458 m/s.

The calculation for the drag is:

Drag = cideal − cmeasured

Drag = 300, 000, 000 m/s − 299, 792, 458 m/s = 207, 542 m/s

13.2 Derivation of the Unresolved Drag Constant (ℵU)

This derivation calculates the fundamental, unresolved "drag" of the vector

field itself. It represents the inherent resistance or "Cost of Being" for

traversal, independent of our perception.

100

13.2.1 1. Define the Core Quantities

Based on the principles of the hypothesis:

– Ideal Speed (cideal): The theoretical, unhindered speed of propagation

in the vector field. cideal = 300, 000, 000 m/s

– Measured Speed (cmeasured): The actual, observable speed of light

in a vacuum. cmeasured = 299, 792, 458 m/s

– Drag Speed (vdrag): The difference between the ideal and measured

speeds. vdrag = cideal − cmeasured vdrag = 300, 000, 000 −

299, 792, 458 = 207, 542 m/s

13.2.2 2. Calculate the Unresolved Drag Constant (ℵU)

The Unresolved Drag Constant is the fundamental ratio of the Drag Speed

to the Ideal Speed. This makes it a property of the field itself, not our

measurement of it.

– Formula: ℵU = vdrag

cideal

– Calculation: ℵU = 207,542

300,000,000 ℵU ≈ 0.0006918066...

13.2.3 3. Conclusion and Physical Meaning

The theory predicts a fundamental, dimensionless Unresolved Drag

Constant of approximately: ℵU ≈ 0.0006918

This value has a profound physical meaning within the model. It is the

direct, physical manifestation of the fundamental opposition that all propagating

energy has with the universal boundary of \*\*2c space\*\*. For every

1 unit of distance an ideal, unhindered wave \*would\* travel, it falls short

by approximately 0.0006918 units due to this inherent opposition. This

constant represents the fundamental "stickiness" or "drag" of reality itself.

13.3 The Goal: Finding Figures for G and k

The Vector Field Theory redefines the fundamental constants of nature

not as arbitrary "fudge factors," but as geometric conversion factors that

describe the mechanics of the underlying vector field. This exploration

aims to find numerical values for these geometric constants based on the

principles of the theory and known experimental data, specifically recognizing

effects within the vector field. At this fundamental level, units containing

exponents (e.g., kg2, m3) are understood as representing singular, linear

interactions (e.g., kg, m).

101

– The Gravitational Constant (G): The theory posits that G is

the factor that converts a unit of mass (M) into the total 2D planar

strain of Planck space that it creates. We will primarily refer to

this as G_planar, with fundamental units conceptually simplified to

m/kg, representing a direct linear strain per unit mass.

– Coulomb’s Constant (k): The theory posits that k is a density

that converts a unit of charge (q) into the total 2D planar strain it

13.4 Deriving the Geometric Gravitational Constant

(G_planar)

We derive a value for G_planar by relating it to the standard, experimentally

measured value of G, now understood as representing a fundamental

appropriate representation for this planar constant before unit simplification.

13.4.1 Method 1: Re-scaling the Standard Constant to a Planar

Value

The standard Gravitational Constant, Gstd, has units of m3 / (kg · s2).

Our geometric constant, Gplanar (prior to unit simplification, Garea), has

units of m2/kg. To convert Gstd to Garea, we scale it by Planck Time

squared (representing an interaction rate) and divide by Planck Length

(to shift from 3D to 2D).

– Proposed Relation: Garea = Gstd × t2

P

lP

– Calculation:

∗ Gstd ≈ 6.674 × 10−11 m3 kg−1 s−2

∗ tP ≈ 5.391 × 10−44 s

∗ lP ≈ 1.616 × 10−35 m

∗ Garea = (6.674 × 10−11) × (5.391×10−44)2

1.616×10−35

∗ Garea = (6.674 × 10−11) × 2.906×10−87

1.616×10−35

∗ Garea = (6.674 × 10−11) × (1.798 × 10−52)

∗ Garea ≈ 1.20 × 10−62 m2/kg

– Conclusion: The geometric gravitational constant (as a planar area)

is approximately 1.20 × 10−62 m2/kg. Conceptually applying the

unit simplification rule, this represents a fundamental planar strain

in m/kg. This incredibly small number has a clear physical meaning:

strained by a single kilogram of mass.

13.5 Exploring the Geometric Electric Constant (k_planar)

magnitude of distortion created by both 0.25 and 0.75 harmonics relative

we empirically observe through phenomena like measured magnetic field

strength.

– Electromagnetism is the much stronger, localized planar strain arising

This "difference" in harmonic interaction, rooted in quantifiable distortions,

13.6 Energy Requirements for Vector Dynamics

13.6.1 Energy for Vectors to "Cross 1c":

The energy required for the constituent vectors of a particle to "cross

1c" (i.e., to break free from their stable, sub-luminal configuration and

propagate at the speed of light) is exactly equal to the particle’s total rest

mass-energy.

105

– Example: The Electron The electron is a stable, sub-luminal "vector

ball" composed of two constituent waves locked in a perfect 0.75

harmonic resonance. Its rest mass is approximately 0.511 MeV. For

the two internal vectors to break free and propagate outwards at ’c’

(as photons), the system must be given enough energy to overcome

its own stable structure. This is precisely what happens in electronpositron

annihilation:

∗ An electron (0.511 MeV) meets a positron (0.511 MeV).

∗ Their opposite geometries cancel out, destroying the stable "vector

ball" structures.

∗ Their combined mass-energy, 1.022 MeV, is released as two photons,

each with an energy of 0.511 MeV, flying off in opposite

directions.

– Conclusion: Thus, the energy required to make the two vectors

inside an electron cross the 1c barrier is precisely 0.511 MeV—the

energy needed to shatter the "cage" that holds them together.
